  <span id="freeText9663265589173877971" style="display:none">Creepy, unsettling, horrific, made me want to sit and fume and hate men for a bit. <br /><br />This book is even scarier than the original movie, although the movie provides more background and explanation as to how the murderous bits would actually work.<br /><br />While some of the organizations and details in the story may be "dated," the core horror of this book is still -- and will always be -- with us: <br />How well do you really know your significant other? <br />If your husband could have the woman of his dreams, would he betray you?<br /><br />It's those very real insecurities that power the fear in this tale.<br /><br /><br />NOTE: If you get the edition with the introduction by Peter Straub <i>do not read that intro first!</i> Not only does he assume you've already read the story, he's pretty tone deaf when it comes to the issue of male/female relations. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ He considered Walter masturbating at the thought of killing and replacing his wife "the funniest revelation" of the book.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>

<div id="review_410694766" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/410694766" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1938544-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1330120106p2/1938544.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/410694766?book_show_action=true">Sep 09, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/1938544-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Horror Fans, Feminists, Satire Fans</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Michael by: </span>
      Bryan Forbes
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1938544-michael?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1938544-michael?shelf=satire">satire</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1938544-michael?shelf=feminism">feminism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1938544-michael?shelf=literature">literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer410694766" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4344409324854891210">As Peter Straub points out in the introduction to this book, a lot of people miss the point. It is not "the easy satire on the banality of suburban housewives that it is commonly taken to be - a misconception that has installed its title in our language as shorthand for those homemakers who affect an uncanny perfection." This resulted in the fact that, after Ann Romney's recent speech at the RNC, I was asked whether I thought she was "like a Stepford Wife." No, she's not. For one thing, her voca</span>
  <span id="freeText4344409324854891210" style="display:none">As Peter Straub points out in the introduction to this book, a lot of people miss the point. It is not "the easy satire on the banality of suburban housewives that it is commonly taken to be - a misconception that has installed its title in our language as shorthand for those homemakers who affect an uncanny perfection." This resulted in the fact that, after Ann Romney's recent speech at the RNC, I was asked whether I thought she was "like a Stepford Wife." No, she's not. For one thing, her vocabulary is too large. But, more than that, referring to politically-active conservative women as Stepford wives undermines the feminist argument of this novella.<br /><br />The critical point here is that this novella is not a parody of the WOMEN of Stepford, it is a parody of their HUSBANDS. As with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/228296.Rosemary_s_Baby" title="Rosemary's Baby by Ira Levin" rel="nofollow noopener">Rosemary's Baby</a>, which is actually about Guy Wodehouse, not Rosemary or her baby, the title here is a deliberate distraction. This book is a humorous critique of the anti-feminist backlash that takes the anti-feminist slogan "War of the Sexes" at its word and suggests that men are so frightened by women's liberation that they will start executing them to prevent it. The fact that our narrator’s husband is “a good guy,” who – at first – treats his wife as an almost-equal only heightens the irony that, when offered a pleasure-android with bigger breasts who will keep the house meticulously clean, he is just as willing as all of the others to kill his own wife to get it. <br /><br />It was interesting to me to see how the book differs from the movie. Unlike most film-adaptations, several scenes have been added to the novella, and fairly little was cut, resulting in a rather long movie (for 1975 sci fi, anyway). There is even the addition of a surprising new character with the unlikely name of Raymond Chandler who isn't in the book. It also seems to me that the truth of the “Men’s Association” is lost in the film: that it isn’t a long-standing organization with an archaic membership policy, but it was a recent innovation founded to combat a growing feminist presence in Stepford after <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/21798.Betty_Friedan" title="Betty Friedan" rel="nofollow noopener">Betty Friedan</a> gave a well-received speech. Also unusual for a film-adaptation, the movie has more explicit sexual references than the book does, and plays up that side of what the men are “up to” while the book leaves this largely to the reader’s imagination. <br /><br />At any rate, the premise of this book is disturbing, and it is intended to be. The prose is efficient and the pacing effective. At its short length, it is a quick read and may actually comment more deeply on American society than it is generally given credit for doing.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4344409324854891210" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1581329607" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1581329607" />
    <a title="Zuky the BookBum" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/37830213-zuky-the-bookbum"><img alt="Zuky the BookBum" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1580914187p2/37830213.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1581329607?book_show_action=true">Jun 05, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zuky the BookBum" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zuky the BookBum" href="/user/show/37830213-zuky-the-bookbum">Zuky the BookBum</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37830213-zuky-the-bookbum?shelf=5-stars">5-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37830213-zuky-the-bookbum?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37830213-zuky-the-bookbum?shelf=2018">2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37830213-zuky-the-bookbum?shelf=book-bum-club">book-bum-club</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1581329607" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6916812042038439354">I read this novel as my BookBum Club book for June. Check out my BookClub on the Goodreads Groups!<br/><br/>I never normally read a lot of one author’s work, unless I feel there is something exceptional there. Last year, my favourite book was Rosemary’s Baby by Ira Levin, so I instantly wanted to pick his work back up again, but I got side-tracked. Finally, thanks to the wonderful Inside My Library Mind, I’ve picked up my second book of his, and wow, whaddya know? It’s another 5 star read!<br/><br/>I am amazed at t</span>
  <span id="freeText6916812042038439354" style="display:none">I read this novel as my BookBum Club book for June. Check out my BookClub on the Goodreads Groups!<br /><br />I never normally read a lot of one author’s work, unless I feel there is something exceptional there. Last year, my favourite book was Rosemary’s Baby by Ira Levin, so I instantly wanted to pick his work back up again, but I got side-tracked. Finally, thanks to the wonderful Inside My Library Mind, I’ve picked up my second book of his, and wow, whaddya know? It’s another 5 star read!<br /><br />I am amazed at the skill of Levin’s writing. He’s one of those authors that doesn’t pepper his novel with the unnecessary, it’s very simply written but not in a juvenile way. It’s hard to explain but it’s the like Goldilocks zone for writing. It’s so easy to follow the story with no distractions but riveting enough to keep you interested. I was honestly breathless with fear and tension reading this novel. How is it that Levin could write so simply, yet produce so much emotion in his readers?<br /><br />Where Shirley Jackson is the Queen, Ira Levin is the King at writing about the mundane with an underlying sense of terror. As this book progresses, and Joanna begins to realise what is happening in her strange little community, there is a slow creeping sense of dread that comes along with it. As you’re both slowly enlightened, your stomach gets hollow with fear.<br /><br />The story in this novel is one pretty much everyone knows now, so reading this in the current age, the plot twist is predictable. However, you have to give Levin credit for basically inventing the creepy neighbourhood vibe that’s so prominent in books nowadays. There’s the twist, and then there’s the ending of this book, two totally different things and both equally as shocking. I love how Levin ends his books.<br /><br />Spoilers??? While I’ve only read two of Levin’s books, I can already see a trend in his work. Men are the bad guys. This was a really interesting (and dramatic) take on the rise of female empowerment and how it made some men feel in the 70’s.<br /><br />This is a really short book at just over 100 pages but it was an absolute rollercoaster! This isn’t a classic horror novel, but it’s very psychologically disturbing, similar to Rosemary’s Baby, although I feel like that one was more terrifying. I really cannot recommend picking up Levin’s work enough. He’s definitely a favourite author of mine.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6916812042038439354" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1131966089" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1131966089" />
    <a title="Christina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/844081-christina"><img alt="Christina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1587328423p2/844081.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1131966089?book_show_action=true">Dec 14, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Christina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christina" href="/user/show/844081-christina">Christina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1131966089" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2872011147509896046">A book so good that it became a cultural reference. Today, everyone knows what a Stepford Wife is. <br/><br/>Ira Levin is a master of the horror genre. This book, while a little sci-fi, reads much more as horror. Talk about unputdownable - I feel like Ira Levin invented the term. <br/><br/>This book is about a woman from New York City who, after some difficult life experiences in the city, moves out to an idyllic countryside village at the behest of her husband. He thinks it would be good for her. But Joanna soon d</span>
  <span id="freeText2872011147509896046" style="display:none">A book so good that it became a cultural reference. Today, everyone knows what a Stepford Wife is. <br /><br />Ira Levin is a master of the horror genre. This book, while a little sci-fi, reads much more as horror. Talk about unputdownable - I feel like Ira Levin invented the term. <br /><br />This book is about a woman from New York City who, after some difficult life experiences in the city, moves out to an idyllic countryside village at the behest of her husband. He thinks it would be good for her. But Joanna soon discovers that there is something...VERY WEIRD about all the wives in this town. They are just too perfect. But they also don't seem capable of any interesting thoughts. And what's up with that secret men's club that only the husbands can join? <br /><br />There are two movies made from this book - the really good original from the 70s, and the entertaining but not as substantive modern version with Nicole Kidman. Both are worth watching, but in this case the book really is so much better that you should read it first.<br /><br />I wish I could have read the book when it first came out without knowing the premise, because I bet that was a huge shocker on the level of a first viewing of Psycho. You know those horror books with a creeping sense of dread, where you just KNOW basically what the ending will be but you are just waiting for the big reveal and hoping it will be as horrifying as you think? Well, this book hits that big reveal right out of the park.<br /><br />This is a must-read classic for all the feminists out there (the references to the trailblazing "women's libbers" who were so controversial at the time are now just really fun to read about). Ira Levin does such an amazing job recounting various dynamics in marriage and society, and capturing some peoples' yearnings for the "good old days" when a wife was just in the kitchen baking a casserole. (Nothing wrong with baking casseroles....but what if an entire town of women just did ONLY that all day with no apparent other interests in their lives or ideas in their heads to share?) Yes, people, a man went ahead and wrote one of my very favorite feminist books. <br /><br />Thank you to my Goodreads friend Jannelies for reminding me how great this book is and getting me to pick it up again!</span>
  <a data-text-id="2872011147509896046" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3568594269" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3568594269" />
    <a title="Simona B" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5312173-simona-b"><img alt="Simona B" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1408893128p2/5312173.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3568594269?book_show_action=true">Sep 27, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Simona B" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Simona B" href="/user/show/5312173-simona-b">Simona B</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781849015899" href="/book/show/10326178-the-stepford-wives">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5312173-simona-b?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5312173-simona-b?shelf=1900">1900</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5312173-simona-b?shelf=in-english">in-english</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3568594269" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15717045692336031538">Reading this book is like getting kicked in the chest by a really angry karateka. Rarely have I seen such capacity to arouse strong emotions in so short a book. Levin's writing is no-nonsense and terse, but lively and incisive. And <i>The Stepford Wives</i> isn't just a book about feminism and independence, but also about herd mentality and how the social scenarios we navigate contribute to changing us. A complete and utterly chilling pocket-tale worth at least one reading in everybody's life.<br/><br/><b>That pref</b></span>
  <span id="freeText15717045692336031538" style="display:none">Reading this book is like getting kicked in the chest by a really angry karateka. Rarely have I seen such capacity to arouse strong emotions in so short a book. Levin's writing is no-nonsense and terse, but lively and incisive. And <i>The Stepford Wives</i> isn't just a book about feminism and independence, but also about herd mentality and how the social scenarios we navigate contribute to changing us. A complete and utterly chilling pocket-tale worth at least one reading in everybody's life.<br /><br /><b>That preface though</b><br />I want to take a moment to contemplate the odiousness of the preface written by Chuck Palahniuk. He starts by praising the radicality and strong emancipatory pull of 1970s feminism, and ends up regretting that the world of today is once again full of <blockquote>"glazed pretty dolls wearing their stylish designer outfits--Prada and Chanel and Dolce--swilling their Martinis and flirting, flirting, flirting in their supreme effort to catch a rich husband. Always a <i>rich</i> husband. [...] Bassieres are back, as are girdles, eyelash curlers, perfumed and meticulously shaved underarms. The speculum and the cervix are forgotten. This it seems is progress: women may now choose to be pretty, stylishly dressed, and vapid. [...]<br />Now everyplace is Stepford, but it's okay. It's fine. This is what the modern politically aware, fully awake, enlightened, assertive woman really, really, <i>really</i> wants: a manicure."</blockquote>And this is a rant written by a patronizing, holier-than-thou male if I've ever read one. Now, I've gotten exactly one manicure in the 23 years of my life and it was a hellish enough experience, thank you very much. But I'd be <i>damned</i> if I have to be ashamed of wanting a manicure, a Chanel perfume, or to flirt with a guy, lest <i>men</i> think I'm "vapid." For goodness' sake. We have gone from men wanting their women to be pretty and hausfrau-y because that's what a real woman is, to men dictating that we be rebellious and keep our underarms hairy for the very same reason. And we <i>listen</i> to these conceited know-it-alls.<br />Palahniuk here is (probably) aiming his darts at women who consider the luxuries and guilty pleasures he so contemptuously enumerates as the end goal of their lives. So what? We would never dream of shaming a man whose goal in life is to be, say, a disgustingly rich businessman, even if he has to do shameful, cruel things to get there. You could say, the difference is that a businessman earns that money with his brain's work. A woman who wants to be rich is probably (?) going to do that by catching a rich husband, as Palahniuk would imply given his contempt of the contemporary female population. But what I see in these scenarios is just two different people using different types of intelligence to achieve what they want. As if there didn't exist men who look for rich women to marry. As if the concept of a ruthless businesswoman were unknown to humankind. Why does everything have to be related to what women or men should want or do or behave like or how they should handle their morality? I'm sick and tired of this rhetoric, and all the more so if it is so graciously handed to us in such a self-righteous fashion. <br />If your life dream is to be forever well-dressed, well, I think you should pursue that, whether you're a man or a woman or neither or both. I think that everyone who says you're vapid because of your love for clothes or jewels or pretty things isn't worth a millisecond of your time. <br />Do I think that we should all go for vapid things? I simply think that that's a useless and meaningless touchstone. "Vapid" is relative. I'm a PhD student of literature, and I'm specializing in speculative fiction. A big portion of academia probably still considers this field childish and self-contained, though things are changing. Should I abandon it and start digging into, I don't know, class struggle in Early Modern texts? That may well be a more important area of study, with a view to contemporary concerns. Who knows. But I do know that I can rest easy, because rest assured that there are a thousand other researchers way more brilliant than I am ready to tackle those areas in which I'm not in the least interested.<br />My point is, there is not one thing that is utterly <i>not</i> important, and we as a species are numerous enough that we can safely enough do what we wish, because we don't all wish for the same things. Our wishes should not be tied to the stereotypes others think we fall into (because make no mistake, in that preface, Palahniuk is stereotyping heavily). External observers should not feel entitled to pass judgment on one's dreams or the usefulness of said dreams. They are useful to <i>you</i>, they are meaningful to <i>you</i>, and that, mesdames et messieurs, is f*****g enough. And I believe that the more enlightened of 1970s feminists would say that they fought also for women's right to get a blessed manicure without this action to be read as a renunciation of female dignity. <br /><br />Long story short: Why didn't they hire a <i>woman</i> to write this damned preface?</span>
  <a data-text-id="15717045692336031538" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_825966322" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/825966322" />
    <a title="Joe Valdez" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26876584-joe-valdez"><img alt="Joe Valdez" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585885999p2/26876584.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/825966322?book_show_action=true">Jan 16, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joe Valdez" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joe Valdez" href="/user/show/26876584-joe-valdez">Joe Valdez</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">The Six Million Dollar Man, the Bionic Woman, the Fembots</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26876584-joe-valdez?shelf=sci-fi-general">sci-fi-general</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer825966322&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer825966322" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12418566489180375827">"Satire" may be one of those words everyone has their own definition of. In terms of film, by two favorite satires are <i>Dr. Strangelove, or How I Learned To Stop Worrying and Love the Bomb</i> and <i>Network</i>. In addition to being hysterically funny, I liked how credible they were, using a Life Magazine approach to document the world as it was at that time and then pushing it one step further. <br/><br/>Ira Levin's <i>The Stepford Wives</i> is neither hysterically funny or plausible. Billed as a "satirical thriller", it </span>
  <span id="freeText12418566489180375827" style="display:none">"Satire" may be one of those words everyone has their own definition of. In terms of film, by two favorite satires are <i>Dr. Strangelove, or How I Learned To Stop Worrying and Love the Bomb</i> and <i>Network</i>. In addition to being hysterically funny, I liked how credible they were, using a Life Magazine approach to document the world as it was at that time and then pushing it one step further. <br /><br />Ira Levin's <i>The Stepford Wives</i> is neither hysterically funny or plausible. Billed as a "satirical thriller", it was published in 1972 and has been adapted to feature film twice, by William Goldman in 1975 and Paul Rudnick in 2004. The plot involves Joanna Eberhardt, a semi-professional photographer who relocates to the hamlet of Stepford with her husband Walter and two children. Joanna balances married life with independence and social equality, as women's liberation sweeps the nation. She observes that almost all of the women of Stepford seem stuck in a TV commercial, cleaning floors, cooking meals, looking sexy, and putting their husbands first.<br /><br />Joanna sees red over the Men's Association, a male only club in Stepford that meet each evening at a clubhouse off limits to women. She finds it strange that Stepford doesn't have a female equivalent and agrees to let Walter join when he promises he'll change things from the inside. Meanwhile, she befriends the only women of Stepford who seem to have a brain; a spitfire named Bobbie Markowe and a tennis bunny named Charmaine Wimperis. Katharine Ross, Paula Prentiss and Tina Louise (Ginger from <i>Gilligan's Island</i>) took these roles in the film.<br /><br />If ever there was a novella that could've been scribbled on cocktail napkins, <i>The Stepford Wives</i> is it. There are airplane novels, but this is the first taxi cab novel I've read. The book could be finished on the way to the airport in time to discuss it with your fellow passengers. It's a conversation starter, with no two readers likely to agree on what was going on in Stepford, what happened to the characters or whether the women's movement might provoke some sort of retaliation from men.<br /><br />To grab me by the throat, a good thriller needs credibility on a basic level. Comic thrillers I've read or seen take their plots deadly seriously and spike the punchbowl with wit as needed to sort of have it both ways. Satire, a far more difficult brew, absolutely requires credibility to work, otherwise, it turns to farce or science fiction at best, cartoon at worst. <br /><br />Whatever <i>The Stepford Wives</i> is, Levin never grabbed me by the throat. There's no atmosphere, no dread or tension (so much for the book being a "thriller"). It isn't funny (that eliminates "satire"). The characters aren't endowed with the basic quirks or intelligences to make them interesting or real. What dialogue there is sort of lies there flatly, while Levin skips through a lot of dialogue or plot with quick summaries.<br /><br />I really can't stand joke based books. Here is something I might dislike even more, a one-joke based book. Here is Levin's joke: "What if men were so threatened by the women's movement that they murdered their wives and replaced them with, wait for it, robots! You know, like the audio-animatronic figures at Disneyland?" <br /><br />Levin devotes some copy to Joanna &amp; Walter's sex life that offered a peek at who these characters were and where they were going to end up, and he raised my pulse a bit in the climax, which is creepy for a few sentences. I can honestly say that I was rooting for the robots at that point. As opposed to satire, I found the novel to be more in the category of "so bad it's good", except, it wasn't very good. It's superficial, lazy and boring.<br /><br />There are some amusing questions in the scenario Levin concocted, but the author doesn't have the imagination to explore any of them. Were husbands put in charge of murdering their own wives, or did Stepford hire a professional? Would the cops or family members or pets suspect something was amiss? How do the robots assimilate into society? What happens when one of them requires maintenance? Do HOA dues cover that, or is it extra? These questions and any possible answer is more amusing than anything offered in the book, which I found over-rated and under-developed.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12418566489180375827" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1531348736" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1531348736" />
    <a title="Kim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18948588-kim"><img alt="Kim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1569598891p2/18948588.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1531348736?book_show_action=true">Oct 11, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kim" href="/user/show/18948588-kim">Kim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18948588-kim?shelf=r-r">r-r</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18948588-kim?shelf=four-star-novels">four-star-novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18948588-kim?shelf=read-again">read-again</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18948588-kim?shelf=sci-fi-and-other-creepy-things">sci-fi-and-other-creepy-things</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1531348736" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3133534790721638426"><i>The Stepford Wives</i> is a 1972 novel by Ira Levin, according to what I've read it's a satirical thriller whatever that is. I know I'm going to have to go look that up, but in the meantime I'll say what little I have to say about the book. First, I didn't know it was a book. That rarely happens, usually I knew it as a book first then sometime later a movie comes out, which I never watch because the book was better. I just know it is, books are always better. But this one I saw as a movie way back i</span>
  <span id="freeText3133534790721638426" style="display:none"><i>The Stepford Wives</i> is a 1972 novel by Ira Levin, according to what I've read it's a satirical thriller whatever that is. I know I'm going to have to go look that up, but in the meantime I'll say what little I have to say about the book. First, I didn't know it was a book. That rarely happens, usually I knew it as a book first then sometime later a movie comes out, which I never watch because the book was better. I just know it is, books are always better. But this one I saw as a movie way back in 1970 something or other, and didn't link a book to it until recently. I don't know how it happened, but it did. From what I can remember, which isn't much, the movie stayed pretty close to the book, and I liked the book. It was fun. I was surprised early in the book that any man would want a wife who is always dressed like every other lady in the town, and who's conversations would have something to do with waxing floors, baking bread, and the best buys they got that day at the supermarket. You would have to get extremely bored in a very short time I would think, but I'm not a man living in Stepford. And I'm not big on housekeeping conversations. A few years ago while waiting for choir practice to begin some of the other women around me were talking about ironing and the best iron to use to do the ironing in the first place. This managed to turn into quite the conversation with everyone having an opinion on heat settings, to steam or not to steam, cooling, heating, all kinds of other iron topics and I sat there calmly looking through the music rather astonished these ladies could keep this conversation going. Finally, one lady decided it was time to draw me into the iron debate and asked what type of iron I use to which I replied, "white". That's all I knew about it and that's still all I know about it. Or want to. So, no, I wouldn't make a very good Stepford wife. <br><br>A few days ago I came across something that seems to fit the Stepford wife, sort of an instruction manual. It's from the 1950's and if you want to try it out, here you go:<br><br><i>The 1955 Good House Wife's Guide<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1507764723i/24146632._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Rule 1 – Have dinner ready. Plan ahead, even the night before, to have a delicious meal ready, on time, for his return. This is a way of letting him know that you have been thinking about him, and are concerned about his needs. Most men are hungry when they come home,<br>and the prospect of a good meal (especially his favorite dish) is part of the warm welcome needed.<br><br>Rule 2 – Prepare yourself. Take 15 minutes to rest so you’ll be refreshed when he arrives. Touch up your make-up, put a ribbon in your hair, and be fresh-looking. He has just been with a lot of work-weary people.<br><br>Rule 3 — Clear away the clutter. Make one last trip though the main part of the house just before your husband arrives.<br><br>Rule 4 — Gather up schoolbooks, toys, paper etc. and then run a dust cloth over the tables.<br><br>Rule 5 — Over the cooler months of the year, you should prepare and light a fire for him to unwind by. Your husband will feel he has reached a haven of rest and order, and it will give you a lift, too. After all, catering for his comfort will provide you with immense personal satisfaction.<br><br>Rule 6 — Prepare the children. Take a few minutes to wash the children’s hands and faces (if they are small), comb their hair and, if necessary, change their clothes. They are little treasures, and he would like to see them playing the part. Minimize all noise. At the time of his arrival, eliminate all noise of the washer, dryer, and vacuum. Try to encourage the children to be quiet.<br><br>Rule 7 — Be happy to see him.<br><br>Rule 8 — Greet him with a warm smile and show sincerity in your desire to please him.<br><br>Rule 9 — Listen to him. You may have a dozen important things to tell him, but the moment of his arrival is not the time. Let him talk first remember, his topics of conversation are more important than yours.<br><br>Rule 10 — Make the evening his. Never complain if he comes home late, or goes out to dinner, or other places of entertainment without you. Instead, try to understand his world of strain and pressure and his very real need to be at home and relax.<br><br>Rule 11 — Your goal: Try to make your home is a place of peace, order, and tranquility where your husband can renew himself in body and spirit.<br><br>Rule 12 — Don’t complain if he comes home late to dinner, or even if he stays out all night. Compare this as minor compared to what he may have gone through that day.<br><br>Rule 13 — Make him comfortable. Have him lean back in a comfortable chair or lie down in the bedroom. Have a cool or warm drink ready for him.<br><br>Rule 14 — Arrange his pillow and offer to take off his shoes. Speak in a low, soothing and pleasant voice.<br><br>Rule 15 — Don’t ask him questions about his actions or question his judgment or integrity. Remember, he is the master of the house, and as such, will always exercise his will with fairness and truthfulness. You have no right to question him.<br><br>Rule 16 — A good wife always knows her place.</i><br><br>I am almost tempted to try one or two of these things just to see the look on my husband's face when he comes home. Oh well, I guess I'll never be a Stepford wife. <br><br>And that's all I have to say, back to reading. Oh, but first I have to go look up what a satirical thriller is. Happy reading. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3133534790721638426" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

This is more sci fi, and here the conspiracy is bigger (the whole town!), but they both involve the husband using her body for his own happiness and success.</span>

<div id="review_1530028935" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1530028935" />
    <a title="Cheryl" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22026875-cheryl"><img alt="Cheryl" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1373374354p2/22026875.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1530028935?book_show_action=true">Jan 29, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cheryl" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cheryl" href="/user/show/22026875-cheryl">Cheryl</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1530028935" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer101205637980524762">It's so hard to rate this book. I've seen the 1975 film version. I knew what to expect from the plot, so it didn't have any real surprises for me. However, I do think the book is worth reading. It really needs to be read as a historical novel set in 1972, as I don't think it's as pertinent to how society is today - at least in the U.S. . I think it played on the concerns of women at that time, as their role in the family was starting to change.<br/><br/>I think the book is alot more ambiguous than the fil</span>
  <span id="freeText101205637980524762" style="display:none">It's so hard to rate this book. I've seen the 1975 film version. I knew what to expect from the plot, so it didn't have any real surprises for me. However, I do think the book is worth reading. It really needs to be read as a historical novel set in 1972, as I don't think it's as pertinent to how society is today - at least in the U.S. . I think it played on the concerns of women at that time, as their role in the family was starting to change.<br /><br />I think the book is alot more ambiguous than the film. We're shown so little of the men of the town, and when we do see them, they really aren't doing anything menacing or threatening. Everything is based on observing the women of Stepford and how they change. Although there is a change in Joanna at the end, we're not told conclusively how it came about, like we are shown in the film. But whether this change is due to madness or to a secret agenda by the men in the town, either explanation is frightening. Joanna is no longer who she was. A short but interesting read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="101205637980524762" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2767193146" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2767193146" />
    <a title="Susan&#39;s Reviews" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19736308-susan-s-reviews"><img alt="Susan&#39;s Reviews" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507651790p2/19736308.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2767193146?book_show_action=true">Mar 28, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Susan&#39;s Reviews" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Susan&#39;s Reviews" href="/user/show/19736308-susan-s-reviews">Susan&#39;s Reviews</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2767193146" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12280792574100805224">My goodness, I read this so, so long ago. Being the staunch feminist that I was and still am, I was furious at the ending. Since then, I have never liked negative or hopeless endings. Still, very well written, and Ira Levin was a master of the game back then.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3161223846" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3161223846" />
    <a title="Roman Clodia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/56111743-roman-clodia"><img alt="Roman Clodia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1464021031p2/56111743.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3161223846?book_show_action=true">Jan 26, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roman Clodia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roman Clodia" href="/user/show/56111743-roman-clodia">Roman Clodia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780739443323" href="/book/show/112721.The_Stepford_Wives">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3161223846" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9684839808264949200"><i>
  <blockquote>"Yes, I've changed. I realised I was being awfully sloppy and self-indulgent. It's no disgrace to be a good homemaker. I've decided to do my job conscientiously, the way Dave does his, and be more careful about my appearance."</blockquote>
</i><br/>Ok, there's nothing subtle about this but it is a hilariously camp satire of small-town conservatism and its masculine backlash against 1960s feminism. It's no surprise to find Kate Millett and Betty Friedan name-checked within the narrative, and it's also a revealing p</span>
  <span id="freeText9684839808264949200" style="display:none"><i>
  <blockquote>"Yes, I've changed. I realised I was being awfully sloppy and self-indulgent. It's no disgrace to be a good homemaker. I've decided to do my job conscientiously, the way Dave does his, and be more careful about my appearance."</blockquote>
</i><br />Ok, there's nothing subtle about this but it is a hilariously camp satire of small-town conservatism and its masculine backlash against 1960s feminism. It's no surprise to find Kate Millett and Betty Friedan name-checked within the narrative, and it's also a revealing portrait of a historicised ideal of womanhood (please tell me it's historicised...): beautiful, busty, empty-headed, obsessed with housework and domestic labour.<br /><br /> It's interesting to note that Walter is changed by Stepford as much as Joanna, being acculturated into being a Stepford husband as a precursor to his wife's 'transformation'. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Are wives only changed once they have provided children for their husbands? We are shown that Joanna and Walter have a good sex life before, but what about after?  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br /><br />So this is short and sharp rather than deep but it still stands up nearly 50 years after it was published, however much gender roles may have progressed. The prose is straightforward, the narrative pacy and engaging, and there's an overblown simplicity about it that keeps the book entertainingly edgy and pointed rather than preachy. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9684839808264949200" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('PiF8PIlhIKezrIQpjZRDSYsXAiSLBAssEHf4brA+cnztAzFab9xNBi2YfjBYAj1CcJ/JRNzp8KdbEaL959Ay1Q==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('9IEQrk5yyDge4bOY3LLLWEFyREHK1Sj6JVbeSF0AifMno13IqM+lmYDVSYEJJLVTuvqPIZ0403FuMITbCu7JWg==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/8050.Ira_Levin">About Ira Levin</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/8050.Ira_Levin"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1195044387p3/8050.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/8050.Ira_Levin">Ira Levin
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
1,138
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer7998760886271084206">Levin graduated from the Horace Mann School and New York University, where he majored in philosophy and English.<br/><br/>After college, he wrote training films and scripts for television.<br/><br/>Levin's first produced play was <i>No Time for Sergeants</i> (adapted from Mac Hyman's novel), a comedy about a hillbilly drafted into the United States Air Force that launched the career of Andy Griffith. The play was turned int</span>
  <span id="freeText7998760886271084206" style="display:none">Levin graduated from the Horace Mann School and New York University, where he majored in philosophy and English.<br /><br />After college, he wrote training films and scripts for television.<br /><br />Levin's first produced play was <i>No Time for Sergeants</i> (adapted from Mac Hyman's novel), a comedy about a hillbilly drafted into the United States Air Force that launched the career of Andy Griffith. The play was turned into a movie in 1958, and co-starred Don Knotts, Griffith's long-time co-star and friend. <i>No Time for Sergeants</i> is generally considered the precursor to <i>Gomer Pyle, USMC</i>. <br /><br />Levin's first novel,<i> A Kiss Before Dying</i>, was well received, earning him the 1954 Edgar Award for Best First Novel. <i>A Kiss Before Dying</i> was turned into a movie twice, first in 1956, and again in 1991.<br /><br />Levin's best known play is <i>Deathtrap</i>, which holds the record as the longest-running comedy-thriller on Broadway and brought Levin his second Edgar Award. In 1982, it was made into a film starring Christopher Reeve and Michael Caine.<br /><br />Levin's best known novel is <i>Rosemary's Baby</i>, a horror story of modern day satanism and the occult, set in Manhattan's Upper West Side. It was made into a film starring Mia Farrow and John Cassavetes. Ruth Gordon won an Oscar for Best Actress in a Supporting Role for her performance. Roman Polanski, who wrote and directed the film, was nominated for Best Writing, Screenplay Based on Material from Another Medium.<br /><br />Other Levin novels were turned into movies, including <i>The Boys from Brazil</i> in 1978; <i>The Stepford Wives</i> in 1975 and again in 2004; and <i>Sliver</i> in 1993. <br /><br />Stephen King has described Ira Levin as "the Swiss watchmaker of suspense novels, he makes what the rest of us do look like cheap watchmakers in drugstores." Chuck Palahniuk, in , calls Levin's writing "a smart, updated version of the kind of folksy legends that cultures have always used."<br /><br />Ira Levin died from a heart attack at his home in Manhattan, on 12 November 2007. He was seventy-eight at the time of his death.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7998760886271084206" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
